ÅšRÄ€DDHA

This subject is of great practical importance from several points of view.

Definition of Å›rÄddha

The BrahmapurÄá¹‡a defines Å›rÄddha as follows: ‘whatever is given with faith to brÄhmaá¹‡as intending it to be for the (benefit of) pitá¹›s at a proper time, in a proper place, to deserving persons and in accordance with the prescribed procedure is called Å›rÄddha1. The Mit. on Yaj. I. 217 defines Å›rÄddha as ‘abandonment with faith of an article of food or some substitute thereof, intending it for (the benefit of) the departed’. The Kalpataru on Å›rÄddha defined it as ’the giving up of sacrificial material intending it for pitá¹›s and its acceptance by brÄhmaá¹‡as’. The ÅšrÄddhaviveka of Rudradhara and the ÅšrÄddhaprakÄÅ›a define Å›rÄddha in the same way as the Mit, but in a more involved manner. Yaj. I. 268 (= AgnipurÄá¹‡a 163. 40-41) states that the pitá¹›s viz. Vasus, Rudras and Ä€dityas that are the deities of Å›rÄddha, being gratified by Å›rÄddha, give gratification to the ancestors of human beings. This verse and Manu III. 284 make it clear that the three ancestors of a man, viz, the father, paternal grand-father and the paternal great grand-father are respectively to be identified with the three orders of superintending pitá¹› deities, viz. Vasus, Rudras and Ä€dityas, when performing Å›rÄddha. According to some2, Å›rÃ¤ddha denotes three things, viz. homa, the offering of piá¹‡á¸a (ball of cooked food) and gratification of brÄhmaá¹‡as invited to a dinner; the application of this word to any one of these three can only be in a secondary sense.

A firm believer in the doctrine of karma, punarjanma (reincarnation) and karma-vipÄka (explained above) may find it difficult to reconcile that doctrine with the belief that by offering balls of rice to his three deceased paternal ancestors a man brings gratification to the souls of the latter. According to the doctrine of punarjanma (as very clearly and succinctly put in Br. Up. IV. 4.4 and Bhagavad-gitÄ 2.22)3 the spirit leaving one body enters into another and a new one. But the doctrine of offering balls of rice to three ancestors requires that the spirits of the three ancestors even after the lapse of 50 or 100 years are still capable of enjoying in an ethereal body the flavour or essence of the rice balls wafted by the wind. Further, YÄj. I. 269 (which is the same as MÄrk. 29. 38, Matsya-purÄÅ†a 19. 11-12, AgnipurÄá¹‡a 163. 41-42) provides that the grand fathers (i.e. pitá¹›s) being themselves gratified (by the offerings of food in Å›rÄddha) bestow on men (their descendants ) long life, progeny, wealth, learning, heaven, mokÅŸa (final beatitude), all happiness and kingdom. In the Matsya-purÄÅ†a (chap. 19, verse 2) a question is asked by the sages how food which a brÄhmaá¹‡a (invited at a Å›rÄddha) eats or which is offered into fire is enjoyed by departed spirits that might have assumed (after death) good or evil forms of bodies. The answer given ( verses 3-9) is that fathers, grand-fathers and great-grand fathers are identified with Vasus, Rudras and Ä€dityas respectively according to Vedic passages, that the name and gotra (mentioned at the time of Å›rÃ¤ddha), the mantras uttered and faith carry to the pitá¹›s the offerings made, that if one’s father has become a god (by his good deeds) the food offered in Å›rÄƒddha becomes nectar and follows him in his state of godhood, if he has become a daitya (an asura) then (the food) reaches him in the form of various enjoyments, if he has become a beast then it becomes grass for him and if he has become a snake the Å›rÄddha food waits on him as wind (serpents are supposed to subsist on wind) and so on.

Verses 5-9 of the Matsya, chap. 19 are quoted as from MÃ¤rkandeyapurÄá¹‡a by the SrÃ¤ddhakalpalata p. 5. VisvarÅ«pa4 on Yaj. I. 265 (p. 171 of Tri, ed.) also raises the same objection and gives several replies. One is that this is a matter entirely based on Å›Ästra and so when sÄstra says that pitá¹›s are gratified and the performer gets desired objects no objection should be raised. Another reply is that the gods Vasus and others that have access everywhere have the power to gratify pitá¹›s wherever they may be situated. He does not call the questioners (nÄstika) as some other and later writers do.

The ÅšrÄddhakalpalata of Nandapaá¹‡á¸ita5 (about 1600 A. D.) enters upon an elaborate reply to these persons (whom he dubs atheists) that aver that the performance of Å›rÄddhas for departed fathers and the rest, who according to the particular actions of each go to heaven or hell or to other forms of existence serves no purpose. He asks: why is Å›rÄddha useless? Is it because there is no prescriptive text laying down an obligation to perform it or is it because Å›rÄddha produces no consequences or is it that it is not proved that pitá¹›s and the rest are gratified by Å›rÄddha? To the first he replies that there are such passages as ’therefore a wise man must perform Å›rÄddha with all his efforts that lay down the obligation; nor is the 2nd objection proper, since YÄj. I. 269 does declare the rewards (of Å›rÄddha ) viz, long life &c. Nor is the third alternative acceptable. In the Å›rÄddha rites it is not that the mere ancestors named Devadatta and the like are the recipients and that they are denoted by the words pitá¹›, pitÃ¤maha and prapitÄmaha, but that those words denote them as accompanied by the superintending deities viz. Vasus, Rudras and Ä€dityas. Just as by the words Devadatta and the like what is denoted is not merely the bodies ( so named) nor merely the souls, but what is denoted by the words is individual souls as particularised by the bodies; in the same way the words pitá¹› and the like denote Devadatta and others together with the superintending deities (viz. Vasus, Rudras and Ä€dityas). Therefore, the superintending deities viz, the Vasus and the rest, being gratified by the food and drink offered by the sons and the rest, gratify those also viz. Devadatta and the rest and endow the performers (of Å›rÄddha ) with such rewards as male progeny and the rest. Just as a woman expecting to be a mother becomes gratified by partaking of the food and drink for which she has a longing in pregnancy and which is given to her by another person for the sustenance of the child in the womb, she satiates also the child in her womb and endows those that offer her the food and drink for which she has longings by bestowing on them some reward in return. Thus the pitá¹›s denoted by the words father, grandfather and great-grand-father are the deities Vasus, Rudras and Ä€dityas, and not merely (human beings called ) Devadatta and the rest. Hence these deities of Å›rÄddha become the recipients (of gifts) in the Å›rÄddha rite, are gratified by the Å›rÄddha and gratify in their turn the ancestors of human beings.

Does Å›rÄddha serve no purpose

The ÅšrÄddha-kalpalatÄ then quotes 18 verses6 from the MÄrkandeyapurÄá¹‡a many of which are found in chap. 28 ( verses 3 ff) of the printed text. It is said7 that just as a calf finds its own mother from among many cows that are scattered about, so the mantras repeated in Å›rÄddha carry the food to the pitá¹›s.

The explanation offered by the ÅšrÄddha-kalpalatÄ relying on passages of the MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eyapurÄá¹‡a is not satisfactory and is rather far-fetched. The MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya and the Matsya appear to agree with the doctrine of VedÄnta that immediately on leaving one body the soul has recourse to another body, either as a god or a man or a beast or a snake &c. The hypothesis propounded is that the food and drink offered in Å¡rÄddhas becomes transformed into various substances for the use of the ancestors (Matsya 141. 74â€“75). But the great difficulty in accepting this explanation is that the ancestors might die at different places, while Å›rÄddha may very often be performed at one place far away from those places. It is difficult to believe that the grass growing in one place where the ancestor has been transformed into a beast as a result of his evil actions is the same that might have been produced from the substances offered in Å›rÃ¤ddha at a place hundreds of miles away. Further, if one or all the three ancestors have been transformed into beasts or the like how can they recognize their offspring and bestow on them long life, wealth &c? If the Vasus, Rudras and Ä€dityas bestow these, it is better to say so directly and affirm that pitá¹›s cannot bestow any thing on their progeny.

Theory of ÅšrÄddha examined

It appears very probable that the worship of ancestors by means of Å›rÄddhas was a very ancient institution and that the doctrines of punarjanma and karmavipÄka were comparatively later ones and that Hinduism being all-embracing retained the institution of Å›rÄddhas while adopting also the doctrine of metempsychosis. The institution of Å›rÄddha is from one point of view an excellent one. It provides an occasion for remembrance of one’s ancestors and relatives that were dear and near when living. The Ä€ryasamÄja objects to the institution of Å›rÄddha and interprets pitá¹›s in the á¹šgveda as meaning living men in the VÄnaprastha stage. It may be noted that the texts support both views. The Å at. Br. expressly says that food is offered to the father of the sacrificer in the words ’this is for thee.’ Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 75. 4 “He whose father is dead may put down a piá¹‡á¸a for his father &c”. On the other hand Manu III, 284 states that fathers are spoken of as Vasus, grandfathers as Rudras &c. and YÄj. I. 269 provides that Vasus, Rudras and Ä€dityas are the pitá¹›s and the devatÄs of Å›rÄddha. These latter are to be explained as containing an injunction to contemplate upon the pitá¹›s as Vasus, Rudras &c.

As stated below (p. 347) with regard to the á¹šgvedic passages, it was on account of the supposed power of pitá¹›s to benefit or harm the living that the cult of the dead became a prominent feature in primitive societies. Offerings and ceremonies which may have in most ancient times been prompted in part at least by the desire to placate the ancestors are continued as tokens of pure affection and remembrance. Various beliefs about pitá¹›s are mentioned8 in post-Vedic Literature. The Baud. Dh, S. II. 8. 14 summarizes a brÄhmaá¹‡a text stating that pitá¹›s move about in the form of birds. The AuÅ¡anasa-smá¹›ti and Devala quoted by the Kalpataru say the same thing.

In the VÄyupurÄá¹‡a[^759] it is stated that at the time of Å›rÃ¤ddha the ancestors enter the brÄhmaá¹‡as (invited) after assuming an aerial form and that when the best of brÄhmaá¹‡as are honoured with clothes, foods, gifts, eatables, liquids, cows, horses and villages, pitá¹›s become pleased. Manu III. 189 and the AuÅ¡anasa-smá¹›ti also support this notion that pitá¹›s enter the invited brÄhmaá¹‡as. The MatsyapurÄá¹‡a (18. 5-7 ) enjoins: piá¹‡á¸as should be offered to the departed for twelve days after death, since they serve hin as food on his journey and give him great satisfaction. Therefore, the soul leaving the dead body is not taken to the abode of the departed for twelve days after death); the departed spirit hovers near his house, his sons, his wife for twelve days. Therefore for ten days after death milk (and water) should be placed (hung up) in space for ten nights for reducing all torments (or troubles of the departed) and for the removal of the fatigue of the journey (that the departed spirit has to make ). The Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasÅ«tra9 (20.34-36 ) provides â€œthe departed spirit enjoys in the world of pitá¹›s the food offered in Å›rÄddha with the utterance of the word ‘svadhÄ’; whether the departed is in the state of a god or in the place of torments (Hell) or in the form of a lower animal or a human being, the Å›rÃ¤ddha food offered by his relatives reaches him; when Å›rÄddha is performed, the performer and the departed soul both certainly secure vigour (or prosperity ).â€

The BrahmapurÄÅ†a10 states that Å›rÄddha is to be treated of under five heads, viz. how, where, when, by whom and with what materials. But before proceeding to deal with these five heads, it is necessary to dilate upon the underlying ideas and significance of the word ‘pitaraá¸¥’ from the most ancient times of which we have literary records.

Meaning of ‘Pitaraá¸¥’

The word ‘pitá¹›’ means ‘father’, but the word ‘pitaraá¸¥’ is used in two senses, viz. (1) a man’s three immediate deceased ancestors, (2) the early or ancient ancestors of the human race that were supposed to inhabit a separate world (loka) by themselves11. For this second meaning, vide á¹šg. X. 14. 2 and 7, X 15.2 (translated above pp. 191-92, 194) and á¹šg. IX.97.3912. “That Soma which becomes stronger and stronger and makes others strong, that is strained through a strainer, that flows in a stream, protected us by means of the luminary (the Sun)–that Soma with whose help our ancestors knowing the place where the cows were kept concealed) and the higher regions, harassed the mountain for the sake of recovering) the cows.’ In á¹šg. X. 15.1 the pitá¹›s are said to be of three grades, lower, middling or higher. They are also said to be earlier and later ones (á¹šg. X. 15.2). They are all known to Agni, though all pitá¹›s are not known to their descendants (á¹šg. X. 15. 13). The pitá¹›s are divided into several groups such as Aá¹…girasas, VairÅ«pas, Atharvans, Bhá¹›gus, Navagvas and DaÅ›agvas (á¹šg. X. 14,5-6), the Angirasas being particularly associated with Yama who is invoked to come to the sacrifice along with the Angirasas (á¹šg. X. 14. 3-5). In á¹šg. I.13 62. 2 it is said: ’through whose (Indra’s) help our ancient ancestors (pitaraá¸¥), the Aá¹…girasas, who sang his praises and who knew the place, found out the cows.’ The pitá¹›s called Angiras were, it appears, again subdivided into two classes viz. Navagva and DaÅ›agya14 both of which words occur in á¹šg. 1. 62 4, V. 39. 12 and X. 62. 6. In several passages the ancient fathers are identified with the seven sages15 as in á¹šg. IV. 42.8 and VI. 22.2 and sometimes the Navagvas and DaÅ›agvas also are said to be the geven sages (á¹šg. I. 62.4). Angirasas are said to be the song of Agni (á¹šg. X. 62, 5) and also of Heaven (á¹šg. IV. 2. 15). The pitá¹›s are often said to regale themselves in the company of gods, particularly of Yama (á¹šg. VII. 76, 4, X, 14, 10, X, 15, 8-10)16. The pitá¹›s are said to be fond of Soma drink (á¹šg. X. 15. 1 and 5, IX. 97. 39), they lie down on kuÅ›a grass (á¹šg. X. 15.5), they come with Agni and Indra to partake of the offerings (á¹šg. X. 15. 10 and X. 16.12) and Agni is also said to carry the offerings to the pitá¹›s (á¹šg. X. 15.12). Fire is supposed to take the spirit of a cremated person to the pitá¹›s (á¹šg. X. 16. 1-2, 5=A, V. 18. 2. 10; á¹šg. X, 17, 3). In later works also (e.g. in MÄrk, chap. 45), BrahmÄ is supposed to have created in the beginning four classes viz. gods, asuras, pitá¹›s and human beings. Vide also BrahmÄá¹‡á¸apurÄÅ†a, PrakriyÄ, chap. 8, and upodghÄta chap. 9. 35 (ityete pitaro devÄ devÄÅ›ca pitaraá¸¥ punaá¸¥ anyonyapitaro hyete).

The World of Pitá¹›s

It was supposed that the departed spirit, after the cremation of the body, was endowed with an ethereal body and became associated with Yama, the gatherer of departed men (á¹šg. X. 14.1 and 8, X. 15. 14, X. 16.5), and with pitá¹›s. The departed spirit went to the world of the pitá¹›s and Agni was implored to take the spirit to the world of the departed whose deeds were good and to the stride of Viá¹£Å†u (á¹šg. X. 14. 9, X. 15.3, X. 16. 4).

Although Yama is said to dwell in heaven (divi) in á¹šg. X. 64.3, he is really a god of the middle region, as the Nirukta states17. The Atharvaveda states:18 ’let us worship with obeisance the fathers and grandfathers of our father, that enter the wide middle regions, that dwell on the earth and in heaven.’ In á¹šg. I. 35.619 it is said : there are three worlds; two of them (Heaven and earth) are in the lap of Savitá¹›; one (i. e. the middle region) is in the domain of Yama where departed spirits congregate, ‘The great Luminary (the Sun) has risen, a gift of the pitá¹›s’ (á¹šg. X. 107. 1). In the Tai. Br. I. 3. 10. 520 it is said that the pitá¹›s dwell in the third world from this. This means that after bhÅ«loka and antariká¹£a comes the pitá¹›loka. In the Br. Up. I. 5.16 three worlds of men, pitá¹›s and gods are separately mentioned. In á¹šg. X. 135. 1-7, Yama is spoken of in somewhat different language. He is in this hymn mentioned as a god by himself and not as the first mortal who made a path (á¹šg. X. 14. 2) or as the gatherer of men (X. 14.1) or as being in company of the pitá¹›s. In a few other places Yama is no doubt called rÄjan and praised in the same breath with Varuá¹‡a (á¹šg. X. 14,7). But such a position is very rarely mentioned. For the further development of Yama and his assistants as the punishers of men for evil deeds, vide pp. 159-160 above and notes 381-386.

There is another division of pitá¹›s viz. pitaraá¸¥ somavantaá¸¥21, pitaraá¸¥ barhiÅŸadaá¸¥, pitaraá¸¥ agniÅŸvÄttÄá¸¥. The latter two are named in á¹šg. X. 15. 4 and 11 (which occur also in Tai. S. II, 6. 12. 2). The Sat. Br. defines these as follows:-“those that performed a soma sacrifice are pitaraá¸¥ somavantaá¸¥; those that offered cooked oblations (like caru and puroá¸ÄÅ›a) and secured a world are pitaraá¸¥ barhiá¹£adaá¸¥; those that did none of these (two actions) and whom fire consumes when burning them are ‘pitaraá¸¥ agniÅŸvÄttÄá¸¥;’ these are the only ones that are pitaraá¸¥.” The Tai.22 Br. has a somewhat similar passage and the KÄthaka Samhita IX. 6. 17 also refers to these three kinds of pitá¹›s. Later writers introduced certain changes in the meanings of the words for the different classes of pitá¹›s and also increased the number of the classes of pitá¹›s. For example, the NandipurÄÅ†a q. by HemÄdri states: the pitá¹›s of brÄhmaá¹‡as are called ‘agniÅŸvÄtta’, those of ká¹£atriyas ‘barhiÅŸadaá¸¥’, those of vaiÅ›yas ‘kÄvyas’, those of sÃ¼dras ‘sukÄlin’ and those of mlecchas and untouchables are called ‘vyÄma’23.

Even Manu (III, 193-198) mentions several classes of pitá¹›s, connects pitá¹›s called SomapÄs, Havirbhujaá¸¥ Ä€jyapÄs and SukÄlins with the four vará¹‡as and in III, 199 states that the pitá¹›s of brÄhmanas are designated as Anagnidagdha, Agnidagdha, KÄvya, BarhiÅŸad, AgniÅŸvÄtta and Saumya. Those verses of Manu appear to summarize different traditions about the several classes of pitá¹›s. Vide MatsyapurÄÅ†a 141. 4 for the same last four names and 141. 15-18 for their definitions. In SÄƒtÄtapasmá¹›ti (VI. 5â€“6) twelve groups or divisions of pitá¹›s are mentioned viz. piá¹‡á¸abhÄjaá¸¥ (three), lepabhÄjaá¸¥ (three), NÄndimukhas (three) and AÅ›rumukhas (three). This is a classification of pitá¹›s from two different standpoints. In VÃ¡yu 72,1 and 73.60, BrahmÄá¹‡á¸a (UpodghÄta 9.53), Padma V. 9. 2-3, Viá¹£á¹‡udharmottara I. 138. 2-3 and other PurÄá¹‡as the classes of pitá¹›s are said to be seven, three of which are formless (amÅ«rtimat) and four have forms (mÅ«rtimat) and they and their offspring are described in detail. All this is passed over here. The SkandapurÄÅ†a (VI. 216. 9-10) speaks of nine groups of pitá¹›s, being AgniÅŸvÄttÄá¸¥, BarhiÅŸadaá¸¥, Ä€jyapÄá¸¥, SomapÄá¸¥, RaÅŸmipÄá¸¥, UpahÅ«tÄá¸¥, those called ‘Äyantunaá¸¥’, ‘ÅšrÄddhabhujaá¸¥, NÄndimukhÄh. In this list old and new elements are mixed up. The Indian mind often revels in divisions, sub-divisions and classifications without much basis therefor and this is probably an illustration of that tendency. Manu (III. 201) states that from the sages the pitá¹›s sprang, from the pitá¹›s sprang gods and human beings and from the gods arose the whole world whether moving or immovable. It is remarkable that here the gods are spoken of as springing from the pitá¹›s. This is really a mere eulogy of pitá¹›s (i. e. it is an arthavÄda).

The pitá¹›s were in a class apart from the gods. On the meaning of the word ‘paÃ±cajanÄá¸¥’ occurring in á¹šg. X.24 53. 4. (paÃ±cajanÄ mama hotram juÅŸadhyam) and other passages, the Ait. Br. (13.7 or 3.31) explains that they are the five classes, viz. Gandharvas with Apsarases, pitá¹›s, devas, sarpas and rÄkÅŸasas. The Nirukta III. 8 partly follows this explanation and also gives another. In the Atharvaveda X, 6.32 the gods, pitá¹›s and men are mentioned in that order. The ancient Vedic texts and practice make a sharp distinction between the Gods and the Pitá¹›s. The Tai. S. VI. 1.1.1 states: ’the gods and men divided the quarters, the gods took the east, pitá¹›s the south, men the west and Rudras the north.’

The general rule is that sacrifices for gods are begun in the forenoon, while the pitá¹›-yajÃ±a is performed in the afternoon (San. Br.)25. The Sat. Br. II, 4.2.2 narrates that the pitá¹›s wearing the sacred thread over the right shoulder (and under the left arm) and bending their left knee approached PrajÄpati, when PrajÄpati said to them ‘you will have food at the end of) each month (on the AmÄvÄsyÄ), your svadhÄ (cordial) will be swiftness of thought and the moon will be your light,’ while to the gods he had said that sacrifice will be their food and the sun their light. The Tai. Br.26 I. 3. 10.4 appears to make a distinction between pitá¹›s who are of the nature and position of gods and pitá¹›s that are more or less like human beings.

The KauÅ›ika-sÅ«tra (1. 9-23) neatly collects in one place the difference in the procedure of the rites meant for gods and for pitá¹›s. The performer of rites for gods wears the sacred thread on the left shoulder and under the right armpit, while in the case of the rites for pitá¹›s it is worn on the right shoulder and under the left arm; the rite for gods is either begun facing the east or north, while that for the pitá¹›s is begun facing the south; the rite for gods is finished in the north-east (or north or east), while that for the pitá¹›s is completed in the south-west; an action is done only once for pitá¹›s, while for gods at least thrice or as many times as the texts direct; in going round (perambulating) the right side is turned towards gods and the left one in the case of pitá¹›s; offerings are made to gods with the words ‘svÄha’ and ‘vaÅŸat’, while they are made to pitá¹›s with ‘svadhÄ’ and ’namaskÄra’; the darbhas employed in rites for Fathers are those that are taken out from the earth with their roots, while for gods darbhas used are cut a little above the roots.

The Baud. Sr. II, 2. also mentions some of these in one place27. The á¹šgveda itself (in X. 14,3’svÄhÄnye svadhayÄnye madanti’) marks this distinction in the words employed at the offerings to Gods and Pitá¹›s. The Sat. Br. (II. 1,3,4 and II. 1.4.9) speaks of the gods as immortal and of the Fathers as mortal.

Though the gods and pitÅ™s are placed in separate classes, still the pitá¹›s partake of some characteristics of the divine. As á¹šg. X. 15. 8 shows, the pitá¹›s drank Soma. In á¹šg. The pitá¹›s are said to have found out the light that was secreted and to have produced the Dawn (á¹šg. VII. 76. 4). Here the pitá¹›s are credited with powers possessed by the highest gods. The pitá¹›s are invoked with affection and regard for conferring various boons and their favour is sought in various ways. In á¹šg. They are requested to grant happiness unmixed with trouble (á¹šg. X. 15. 4.), to bestow wealth on the sacrificer (á¹šg. X. 15.7 and 11) and on his son. á¹šg. X. 15.11 and A. Attitude to Pitá¹›s

It should not be, however, supposed that the element of fear of the pitá¹›s is altogether wanting31. For example, á¹šg. In many passages of the Vedic Literature the word pitaraá¸¥ is applied to the three immediate deceased male ancestors of a man. “Therefore up to three generations they specify (the ancestors) by name; for so many are the ones to whom sacrifice is offered’ (vide n. 772 above). On a passage of the BrahmapurÄÅ†a34 prescribing that the performer should say to the invited brÄhmaá¹‡as that he would call the pitá¹›s to the rites and that when the brÄhmaá¹‡as give permission to call them he should do so, the Åšr. Promulgators of Å›rÄddha rites

From the sÅ«tra period ( about 600 B.C.) to the most modern among medieval DharmaÅ›Ästra works the authors wax eloquent over the praises or the importance or benefits of the institution of Å›rÄddhas. One of the earliest works among these, viz. the Ap. Dh. S. gives the following interesting information :35 â€œFormerly men and gods lived together in this world. The gods went to heaven owing to sacrifices (i. e, as a reward of sacrifices that they performed), but men remained behind. Those among men who perform sacrifices in the same way as the gods did, dwell in the other world (i.e. heaven) with the gods and Brahman. Then (seeing that men lagged behind) Manu promulgated the rite which is designated by the word ‘Å›rÄddha’ and which tends to the salvation (or happiness) of mankind. In this rite the Manes (pitaraá¸¥) are the deities but the brÄhmaá¹‡as (that are fed) are in the place of the Ähavaniya fire (in which in sacrifices to gods oblations are offered)”. On account of this last sÅ«tra Haradatta (com, of Ä€p. Dh, S.) and others hold that feeding the brÄhmaá¹‡as is the principal act at a Å›rÄddha. The BrahmÄá¹‡á¸apurÄÅ†a (UpodghÄtapÄda 9. 15. and 10. 99) speaks of Manu as the promulgator of Å›rÄddha rites and Viá¹£Å†upurÄÅ†a III. 1. 30, VÄyu 44. 38 and BhÄgavata III. 1, 22 designate Manu as ÅšrÄddha-deva, Similarly, in the SÃ¤ntiparva 34536. 14-21 and the Viá¹£á¹‡u-dharmottara I.139.14-16 it is stated that the institution of Å›rÄddha was established in the Boar incarnation by Viá¹£á¹‡u and that Viá¹£á¹‡u should be regarded as dwelling in the three piá¹‡á¸as offered to the father, grand-father and great-grandfather. ÅšrÄddhÄdhikÄrin

Meaning of ‘putra’

It is emphasized85 that he alone deserves to be called putra, who, while the father is alive, obeys his words, and gives plenty of food (to brÄhmaá¹‡as) every year (after his father’s death) and who offers piá¹‡á¸as (to his ancestors) at GayÄ. The general rule laid down was that a child, whose upanayana has not been performed, is like a sÃ¼dra and lacks authority to utter Vedic passages (vide Ä€p. Dh. S, II, 6. 15. 19, Gaut. II, 4-5, Vas. II. 6, Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 28. 40. Manu II, 172)86. But an exception was recognized by almost all these authorities that a son, though his upanayana may not have been performed, can repeat the Vedic mantras required in funeral rites. MedhÄtithi on Manu II, 172 explains that a minor son, even though not initiated into Vedic study by upanayana, can offer water to his father, perform the navaÅ›rÄddhas (mentioned above in n. 593) and repeat such mantras as ‘Å›undhantÄm pitaraá¸¥’ required in these rites, but since he has no Å›rauta or gá¹›hya fire of his own he cannot perform such Å›rÄddhas as the pÄrvaá¹‡a. The Smá¹›tyarthasara (p. 56) provides that boys whose upanayana has not been performed, women and sÃ¼dras should get Å›rÄddhas performed through a priest or they may themselves perform them without mantras but only mention the name and gotra of the deceased or with the two mantras ‘devebhyo namaá¸¥’ and ‘pitá¹›bhyaá¸¥ svadhÄ namaá¸¥’. The above discussion shows that men and women, adults as well as children, those who are upanita as well as those that are anupanÄ«ta are under an obligation to perform Å›rÃ¤ddha.

Tai. S. I. 8. 5.1, Tai. Br. I. 6.9 (which will be quoted later on) show that Å›rÄddha was offered to three immediate ancestors, father, grand-father, great-grand-father. The Baud. Dh. S. (I. 5. 113-115) states that there is a group of seven persons closely knit together that is called avibhaktadÄya sapiá¹‡á¸as viz. great-grand-father, grand-father, father, the man himself (who offers piá¹‡á¸as to the preceding three), his full brothers, his son from a wife of the same caste, the grandson and great grandson; that sakulyas are those that are called ‘vibhaktadÄyÄdas’, that the wealth of the deceased descends to those who are born of the body of the deceased87. Manu IX. 137 (= Vas. 17,5 Viá¹£Å†u Dh. S. 15. 46 ) solemnly 88 affirms: ‘by (the birth of) a son a man wins the worlds (heaven &c.), he secures immortality by a grandson and by the grandson of a son he reaches the abode of the Sun’. This verse shows that all the three descendants of a person confer equally great spiritual benefit on him. YÄj. also (in 1. 78) lumps the three together (without making any distinction between them) when he says since a man secures perpetuation of lineage and heaven by means of son, grand-son and great-grand-son’. Therefore when Manu (IX. 106 ) says that by the birth of a son a man discharges his debt to his progenitors the DÄyabhÄga (XI. 34) explains that the word putra stands for the three descendants up to the great grandson, since all the three are entitled to perform the pÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha and equally benefit the ancestors by the piá¹‡á¸as they offer, and that the word putra is not to be taken literally but only as illustrative in order to include the great-grandson since one may with difficulty find a text expressly mentioning the adhikÃ¤ra of the grandson (as a performer of Å›rÃ¤ddha and taker of wealth) but there is no text expressly mentioning the great-grandson separately (as the taker of wealth and giver of piá¹‡á¸a )89. On YÄj. II. 50 which lays down that when the father dies or has gone to a distant country or is overwhelmed by calamities (incurable diseases or the like) his debts should be paid by the sons or grandsons, the Mit. adds that even when a son or grandson receives no ancestral property the father’s debts must be paid, the only difference being that the son has to pay the principal as well as the interest, while the grandson has to return only the principal and has to pay no interest and the Mit. quotes a verse of Bá¹›haspati90 where all the three descendants are mentioned in one breath and the Mit. adds that the great-grandson has not to pay even the principal if he has received no ancestral property.

This last remark implies that the Mit, regarded that the great-grandson was also included in the extended sense of ‘putra’. YÄj. II. 51 begins by saying that whoever takes the wealth of a deceased person has to pay the latter’s debts, so the great-grandson would have to pay the great-grand-father’s debts if he received the assets of the grand-father. Therefore the Mit. on II. 50 expressly mentions that a great-grandson need not pay his great-grand-father’s debts if he received no assets, because, otherwise being included in the extended meaning of putra, he would have had to pay. There was no necessity to bring in or mention the prapautra under II. 50 if the Mit. did not include the prapautra in the word putra. Again on YÄj. II. 51 (‘putrahinasya rikthinaá¸¥’) the Mit. included even prapautra under the word putra. This shows that the Mit. is quite conscious that the three descendants of a man form one group and succeed to his estate and liabilities and putra includes three descendants wherever the context so requires. If the word ‘putra’ is not to be taken as only illustrative and as including both grandson and great-grandson, serious difficulties will arise even in the interpretation of Yaj. on which the Mit. comments. For example, in YÄj. II. 135-136 it is said that when a gonless man dies his wife, daughters and other heirs (named there ) succeed one after another. If â€˜putra’ is taken to mean only son’ and no one else, then on the death of a man without a son his wife or daughter (whoever is alive ) will take the wealth even if a son’s son exists. But that would be absurd and is not stated by any one. Therefore the word putra has to be interpreted in a wider sense in a proper context. Many works such as the VyavahÄramayukha, the Viramitrodaya, the DattakamimÄngÄ interpret the word ‘putra’ as including three generations. Therefore, though the Mit. in its remarks on inheritance and succession expressly names the son and grandson only (lit. it should have mentioned only the son) it must be taken as including the great-grandson also, particularly in view of the fact that it refers to the great-grandson in its comment on YÄj. I, 50 and 51 as taking the riktha which the Mit. (on YÄj. II, 114) interprets as aptratibandha dÄya. Baud., Manu, YÄjÃ±avalkya speak of the three descendants and Åšankhalikhita, Vas. XI. 39 and Yama employ91 only the word putra or suta in relation to the three ancestors. Therefore, the remark of Dr. Kapadia in ‘Hindu Kinship’on p. 162 ‘VijÃ±Äne Å›vara understands by the word putra sons and grandsons only’ is unjustified. It is impossible to hold with Dr. Kapadia that the Mit. ran counter to the express dicta of renowned Smá¹›tis such as those of Manu, Åšankha-Likhita and Puraá¹‡as like the Viá¹£á¹‡u-purÃ¤á¹‡a III. 13,30 (quoted above in note 582) and that in its remarks on succession it forgot its own interpretation of putra on Yaj. II. 51. He has also not correctly understood the words of the Mit, introducing YÄj. II. 137: ‘sons and grandsons take the wealth; in their absence, the wife and others; thus it is said (by me). This is wrong for several reasons. “Wife and others take the wealth’ is said by YÄj. (in II. 135-36). The following words are ’now (the author) declares an exception to both.’ This clearly refers to YÄj. (II. 137) and not to VijÃ±Äne Å›yara and so ‘uktam’ also must refer to YÄj. and not to VijÃ±ÄneÅ›vara. Therefore, according to the Mit, the great-grand son is included in the extended meaning of the word ‘putra’, is entitled just like a son or a grandson to offer Å›rÄddha (in default of a son or grandson) and to take the wealth and pay the debts of the ancestor. In the matter of payment of debts there is, on account of the difference in the period that elapses, a difference between all the three descendants if no ancestral wealth is left by the ancestor, viz. the son pays the debt with interest, the grandson only the principal lent but without interest and the great-grandson nothing at all. This shows that even between the son and the grandson that are expressly mentioned by Yaj. II. 50 as to the payment of debts there is a difference and that as the great-grandson had to pay nothing when no ancestral property existed he was not mentioned at all in YÄj. II, 50.

Just as the king is an ultimate heir and also the guardian of all minors, so he is like the son of a person (who has no relatives left) for performing Å›rÄddhas.

Times for ÅšrÄddhas

The next question is about the times when Å›rÄddha is to be performed. It has been seen above (vide H, of Dh. vol. II. p. 696 ff) that long before the time of the Sat. Br. five daily observances (called MahÄyajÃ±as) were prescribed for each house-holder, viz. the sacrifice to beings, that to men, that to the Fathers, that to the gods and that to Veda. The Sat. Br. and the Tai. Ä€. (II. 10) say further that this daily rite in which one offers svadhÄ (food) to the Fathers even up to water was called pitá¹›yajÃ±a, Manu III. 70 defines pitá¹›yajÃ±a as tarpaá¹‡a (satiating ancestors with the offerings of water). Manu III. 82 provides that an householder should daily perform a Å›rÄddha with food or with water or with milk, roots and fruits and thus please the Fathers. ÅšrÄddha originally meant a sacrifice performed for the Fathers on AmÄvÄsyÃ¤ (vide Gaut. 15.1-2)92. By applying that word to the daily oftering of water to the Manes what is intended to be conveyed is that the special characteristics of Å›rÃ¤ddha in the strict sense are to be extended to this daily rite so far as possible. AmÄvÄsyÄ is of two kinds, SinivÄli and KuhÅ«. Those who keep Å›rauta fires should perform Å›rÄddha on the former, while those who have not kept Å›rauta fires and sÃ¼dras should offer Å›rÄddha on the latter.

ÅšrÄddhas are divided into three classes, nitya, naimittika and kÄmya. An observance is called nitya when it is laid down that it must be performed on a certain or fixed occasion (such as every day, on an amÄvÃ¤syÃ¤, or on AÅŸá¹­akÄ day). What is laid down for being done on an occasion which is uncertain is called naimittika (such as the birth of a son). What is ordained to be done in case one desires a certain reward or fruit is called kÄmya (e.g. the performance of a Å›rÄddha on Ká¹›ttikÄ or RohiÅ†i by one who desires heaven or progeny ). The five daily yajÃ±as including pitá¹›-yajÃ±a are nitya i.e. they must be performed without any eye to any reward therefrom and the non-performance thereof will lead to sinfulness.

Passages mentioning the rewards that follow from the performance of obligatory ( nitya ) rites are only laudatory, they only convey that such performances make a man pure, but it is not meant that they are not obligatory and may be performed only if a person desires the rewards or results promised (i, e. such performances are not kÄmya). Ä€p. Dh, S. (II. 7. 16. 4-7) provides certain times for the performance of Å›rÃ¤ddha viz, that it must be performed in the latter half of every month, that the afternoon is preferable for it, that the last days of the latter half of each month are preferable to the first days of the latter half. Gaut, 15. 3 and Vas. Kurma (II. 20. 23) says the same thing. The AgnipurÄá¹‡a 115. 8 says that at GayÄ a Å›rÄddha may be performed on any day (na kÄlÄdi GayÄtirthe dadyÄt piá¹‡á¸Äá¹Å›ca nityaÅ›aá¸¥). Meanings of ‘kutapa’

The word kutapa has eight meanings as noted by the Sm. c. (Å›rÄddha) p. 433 and HemÄdri on Å›rÃ¤ddha p. 320. The word is derived from ku’ meaning ‘condemned’ (i.e.sin) and ’tapa’ means ‘what burns.’ The eight105 meanings of ‘kutapa’ are ‘midday, a vessel of rhinoceros horn, woollen seat or blanket from Nepal, silver, darbhas, sesame, cows and daughter’s son.’ The general rule is that Å›rÄddhas are performed in the afternoon (but this applies to Å›rÄddha on New moon, MahÄlaya, Aá¹£á¹­akÄ and Anvaá¹£á¹­akÄ Å›rÃ¤ddhas ), while Vá¹›ddhiÅ›rÄddha and Å›rÄddha in which only corn is offered (ÄmaÅ›rÃ¤ddha) are performed in the morning. MedhÄtithi on Manu III. 245 quotes a smá¹›ti text on this106. The TrikÄá¹‡á¸amaá¹‡á¸ana (II. 150, 162) provides that when it is not possible to perform a religious rite at the time prescribed for it as appropriate, it should be performed at some other time thereafter and that, between the proper time of a rite and the means, the former is to be preferred and one should not wait for a time later than the proper one with the desire of being able to collect all the principal materials107.

                                  The classification of ÅšrÄddhas.

ÅšrÄddhas have been variously classified. One classification, viz. into nitya, naimittika and kÄmya has already been described above (p. 369). That Å›rÄddha is called ‘karmÄnga’ which is performed at the time of the GarbhÄdhÄna rite or at the performance of a soma sacrifice, or at simantonnayana, puá¹savana (it is like VrÌ¥ddhiÅ›rÄddha ). That is called puá¹£á¹­iÅ›rÄddha which is performed for the health (or fattening ) of the body (when taking some medicine for the purpose ) or for the increase of one’s wealth. Out of these119 twelve the principal ones are pÄrvaá¹‡a, ekoddiÅŸá¹­a, vrÌ¥ddhi and sapiá¹‡á¸ana. There are works called Åaá¹‡-á¹‡avati-Å›rÃ¤ddha such as those of Åšivabhatta, son of Govinda, and of RaghunÄtha. The 96 Å›rÃ¤ddhas to be performed in a year are briefly enumerated in a verse120 quoted in the note. They are: 12 Å›rÄddhas on the 12 amÄvÄsyÄs in the year, the four Å›rÄddhas on the YugÄdi days (note 841 above), the fourteen Å›rÄddhas on the ManvantarÄdi days (p. 375 above), the Å›rÄddhas on 12 saá¹…krÄntis (i. e. the sun’s apparent motion from one sign of the Zodiac to another), the 13 Å›rÄddhas on the Yoga called DhrÌ¥ti (i. e. These classifications and enumerations of Å›rÄddhas will afford an idea how the doctrine of Å›rÃ¤ddhas was carried to an exaggerated extent in the course of centuries. It goes without saying that only a few people could have indulged in the luxury of so many Å›rÄddhas a year and most people were content with the performance of only one MahÃ¤layaÅ›rÃ¤ddha and one or two more. It may be noted that Manu (III. 122) first prescribed that on the amÄvÄsyÄ of each month an elaborate Å›rÄddha should be performed, but knowing that this was almost impossible Manu recommends to all (III. 281) that an elaborate Å›rÄddha should be performed on three amÄvÄsyÄs in one year121 (viz. in Hemanta, GrÄ«á¹£ma and VarÈ™Ä ) and one should perform every day the Å›rÄddha which is one of the five daily sacrifices (III. 82-83). Devala went further and recommended that an elaborate Å›rÄddha may be performed only once a year.

Who should be invited at Å›rÄddha

Inquiring into qualifications

The Ná¹›simhapurÃ¤na138 forbids close inquiry into the character and learning of one who comes by chance as a guest at the time of Å›rÄddha. The Viá¹£Å†upurÄÅ†a (III. 18. 70 ff) narrates how a king after a bath in a holy place talked with a heretic and had to pass in consequence through the bodies of a dog, a jackal, wolf, vulture, a crow, a crane and peacock and ultimately became freed from the taint by a bath at the avabhá¹›tha in an AÅ›vamedha sacrifice. The same PurÄá¹‡a provides (III. 18. 97) that one should avoid talking with or touching heretics, particularly at the time of a religious rite or when one has consecrated for a solemn sacrifice. The VÄyupurÄÅ†a144 provides that people who are ’nagna’ should not be allowed to see a Å›rÄddha and then defines “nagna’ people as follows: ’the three Vedas are declared to be a protective covering for all beings and therefore those who foolishly abandon the Vedas are called ’nagna’; those who vainly keep matted hair, those who shave their heads for no purpose, those who are nagna (naked) without any cause, those who engage in certain observances and mutter certain words aimlessly are called ‘nagnÄdi’.

Just as certain countries were declared unfit for the performance of Å›rÄddhas (vide note 853 above), so certain brÄhmaá¹‡as hailing from certain countries were declared in some works as not fit to be invited at a Å›rÄddha145. For example, the MatsyapurÄÅ†a states that brÄhmaá¹‡as that are ungrateful, that are atheists, that stay in Mleccha countries or the countries of TriÅ›aá¹…ku, Karavira, Ä€ndhra, CÄ«na, Draviá¸a and Koá¹…kaá¹‡a should be carefully avoided at the time of Å›rÄddha. HemÄdri (on Å›rÄddha p. 505) quotes from the SaurapurÄá¹‡a the following: ‘BrÄhmaá¹‡as hailing from the countries of Aá¹…ga, Vaá¹…ga, Kaliá¹…ga, SaurÄÅŸtra, (KÄthiawar), Gurjara, Ä€bhira, Koá¹…kaá¹‡a, Draviá¸a, Daká¹£inÄpatha, Avanti and Magadha should be avoided at Å›rÄddhas. It will be noticed that putting the above two passages together, brÄhmaá¹‡as from half of modern India would have been ineligible for being invited at Å›rÄddhas if these passages were literally followed. This was probably a counsel of perfection prompted by the false pride and prejudices of the authors of those works. The ÅšrÄddhaviveka of Rudradhara (pp. 39-41 ) contains one of the longest lists of persons unfit to be invited at a Å›rÄddha.

The following reason is advanced by the VarÄhapurÄá¹‡a146 and others for honouring a guest who comes by chance at the time when a Å›rÄddha rite is in progress: “Yogins wander over the earth assuming different forms that prevent recognition, but doing good to people; therefore a wise man should honour a guest who arrives at the time of the performance of a Å›rÄddha”. The Bhaviá¹£ya-purÄÅ†a147 similarly says that an atithi ( guest ) at a Å›rÄddha is one who comes when least thought of and not one who had arrived before the Å›rÄddha rite started. The MÃ¤rkaá¹‡á¸eya ( 26, 30 ) provides that one should not ask such an atithi about his gotra or caraá¹‡a or his Vedic study nor should one consider whether he has a fine appearance.

Honouring atithi at Å›rÄddha

HemÄdri (on Å›rÄddha pp. 430-433 ) quotes verses from the Sivadharmottara, Viá¹£á¹‡udharmottara and VÄyu (71.74â€“75 ) PurÄá¹‡as that gods, siddhas and yogins wander over the earth in the form of brÄhmaá¹‡a atithis in order to favour people and to see how Å›rÄddhas are being performed. About the definition of an atithi and the necessity and mode of honouring a guest in general, vide H, of Dh. vol. II. pp. 749-756.

HemÄdri (ÅšrÄddhakÄÅ†á¸a, pp. 380-385) holds an interesting discussion as to whether a performer of Å›rÃ¤ddha belonging to one recension of a Veda must restrict himself to inviting brÃ¤hmanas that are students of the same recension of the Veda or whether he can invite any brÄhmaá¹‡a that may have studied any of the three Vedas. Some people relying on a maxim (‘as in the case of a bride so in the case of an offering’) invite only brÄhmaá¹‡as of their own Å›ÄkhÄ possessed of the qualifications above set forth. HemÄdri replies that this is a wrong notion, relies on Ä€p. Dh. S. II. 6. 15.9 ‘one should feed148 in all (religious) acts brÄhmaá¹‡as that are pure (in their conduct) and that have studied the Veda’ and asserts that no smá¹›ti, ItihÄsa, PurÄÅ†a, Gá¹›hyasÅ«tra nor KalpasÅ«tra contains the restriction of inviting only brÄhmaá¹‡as of the performer’s own Å›ÄkhÄ. He further says that in such texts as ’triá¹‡Äciketas trimadhuá¸¥’ (quoted in note 862a above) the rule laid down is to invite brÄhmaá¹‡as that have studied different Å›akhÄs and Vedas. He rejects the idea about any restriction as to selecting a bridegroom of the same Å›ÄkhÄ as the bride’s father and remarks that if some people are not prepared to give their daughters in marriage to young men belonging to other Å›ÄkhÄs, that is due to ignorance about the families and is also due to pride and vanity. He winds up by saying that in Ä€ryÄvarta countries it is found on all sides that marriage connections are entered into with persons studying different Å›ÄkhÄs but living in the same district, (such connections) not being condemned, while persons who study the same Å›ÄkhÄ of the Veda but do not know each other do not enter into marital connections149.

As a contrast to this commonsense view it should be noted that the author of the BÄlambhatti recommends that ‘MahÄrÄstra brÄhmaá¹‡as should not invite brÄhmaá¹‡as of other castes (of brÄhmaá¹‡as ) and particularly the Koá¹…kaá¹‡astha brÄhmaá¹‡as should be avoided and it goes so far as to say that a pergon belonging to one’s own caste not possessed of good qualities and of a blemished character (provided he is not guilty of mahÄpÄtakas or the like) should be preferred to one belonging to another sub-caste even if endowed with good qualities.

The Vas. Dh, S. provides150 that the performer should invite ascetics, house-holders, well conducted men, who are not rery old &c.

If five were invited two would be for gods and three for pitá¹›s; if soven, then four for gods and three for pitá¹›s and so on. Vas. XI. 27 ( = Manu III, 125 = Baud. Dh. S. II. 8. 29), YÄj. Elaborate provisions are made from ancient times about the substances and utensils proper for being used at Å›rÄddha and about those that should not be used therein. Ap. Manu III. 267 ( VÄyu 83.3) is to the same effect as Ap. Dh. S. YÄj. I. 258 is content to say that food which is fit to be offered in sacrifices (havisya) should be served. Manu III, 257 explains that food eaten by hermits in the forest, (cow’s) milk, soma juice, meat that is not prepared with spices (or that is free from a bad smell), rock salt are by their very nature havis (sacrificial food). According to Gaut. 27. 11 the sacrificial food (havis) comprises boiled rice, food obtained by begging, ground barley (after being baked or fried), grain separated from husk, barley gruel, vege tables, milk, curds, clarified butter, roots, fruits and water. 928 Other smá¹›tis and digests very much elaborated these brief indications in the early works. IV)

What milk and water to be used

415

From considerations of space this matter cannot be pursued further. The Mit, on YÄj. I. 240 puts together a long list of allowed and of condemned kinds of grains and other articles.

What milk should be ordinarily partaken of and what should be avoided has been dealt with in H. of Dh, vol. II, pp. 782-783. A few words are added here. Manu III, 271 and YÄj. I. 258 provide that if cow’s milk or rice cooked therein (called pÄyasa) is offered in Å›rÄddha the pitá¹›s are satisfied for one year. VÄyu 78. 17, Brahma 935 220.169, MÃ¤rkandeya 32,17-19, Viá¹£á¹‡upurÄá¹‡a III.16.11 forbid the use in a Å›rÄddha of the milk of a she-buffalo, of a camari or female deer, of sheep or ewes, of she camels, of human females and of all animals with one hoof and the use of the curds and clarified butter prepared from such milk. But ghee prepared from buftalo milk was allowed by Sumantu and Devala (q. by HemÄdri on Å›rÄddha p. 572).

The MÃ¤rkandeyapurÄá¹‡a 936 (29.15-17), VÄyu (78.16) and Viá¹£á¹‡upurÄá¹‡a (III, 16.10) lay down that the water to be employed in Å›rÄddha must not have a bad smell, must not be foamy, or taken from a puddle or a small reservoir which cannot slake the thirst of a cow, must not have been brought overnight, must not be taken from a reservoir not dedicated to all or from a trough or the like meant for beasts.

Several rules are laid down about the fruits, roots and vegetables recommended or condemned for use in Å›rÄddha. For example, the Brahma-purÄÅ†a (220.156-158 ) enumerates several kinds of fruits such as mangoes, bilva, pomegranate, cocoanut, dates, grapes, as fit to be given in Å›rÃ¤ddha. KuÅ¡as are required in Å›rÄddha. Vide H, of Dh. vol. II. p.657 for general remarks on kusas. A few words may be added here. The Sat. Br. Use of flesh in modern times

securing their favour. In course of time that conception grew 361 onger and stronger, so much so that Manu (V. 27-44 and V.46-47) and Vasiá¹£á¹­ha appear to be in two minds (vide H. of Dh. vol. Gradually the offering of flesh in Madhuparka and in Å›rÄddhas came to be totally condemned in works of the 12th and 13th centuries and onwards as a matter prohibited in the Kali age (VarÄtithipitá¹›bhyasca paÅ¡Å«pÄkaraá¹‡akriya). If a person is destitute, some of the PurÄá¹‡as such as the Viá¹£á¹‡u (III. 4.24-30) and the VarÄha (13,53-58) mercifully provide in identical 955 verses that instead of entertaining brÄhmaá¹‡as with a sumptuous dinner or flesh the poor man may offer only un cooked corn, or some vegetables growing wildly or some slight daksinÄ if he cannot offer even uncooked food or vegetables, or he may offer a few grains of sesamum (7 or 8 ) to a brÄhmaá¹‡a with water in his folded hands, or grass to a cow enough for one day and if he cannot afford even any one of these, then he may repair to a thicket of trees, raise his arms so that his armpits are exposed and loudly address the guardians of the worlds such as the Sun in the following words: ‘I have neither wealth nor money nor anything else fit to be offered by way of Å›rÄddha; I bow to my pitá¹›s; may the pitÅ™s be satisfied by my devotion; I have stretched these arms in space!’ (lit, the path of the wind).

He (thereby) pleases the seasons. The seasons are indeed the shining (or divine) pitá¹›s……The darbha grass is cut with one stroke; the pitá¹›s have as it were (passed away) once for ever. He puts down thrice (the piá¹‡á¸as). The pitá¹›s are indeed in the third world from here. He (thereby) pleases them. Piá¹‡á¸apitá¹›yajÃ±a in sat. Br.

The Sat. Br. (II. 4. 2) contains the following somewhat fuller account of the Piá¹‡á¸a-pitá¹›y ajÃ±a, omitting legendary or other irrelevant matters :-When the moon is not seen either in the east or in the west, then he (the performer of the Darsa sacrifice) presents food to the Fathers in each month …… He presents it in the afternoon. The forenoon indeed belongs to the gods; the midday to men; and the afternoon to the Fathers; therefore he presents (food to the Fathers) in the afternoon. While seated behind the GÄrhapatya fire with his face turned towards the south and the sacred thread on his right shoulder he takes that material (for the offering from the cart). There upon he rises from thence and threshes the rice while standing north of the DakÅŸiÅ†a fire and facing the south. Only once does he clean the rice, for it is once for all that the fathers have passed away. He then boils it. While it stands on the DakÅŸiÅ†a fire) he pours some clarified butter on it. After remov ing it (from the fire) he offers to the gods two oblations in the fire …… Here he is engaged in a sacrifice to the Fathers; hence he thereby propitiates the gods and being permitted by the gods, he presents that food to the Fathers. He offers to both Agni and Soma … He offers with the formula To Agni, the carrier of kavya (what is offered to the Manes), svÄhÄ!’, ‘To Soma accompanied by the Fathers, svÄhÄ!’ (VÄj. S. II 29). He then puts the pot-ladle (mekÅŸana) on the fire, that being in lieu of the SvistakÅ™t. Thereafter he draws (with the sphya, the wooden sword) one line south of the DakÅŸiÅ†a fire, that being in lieu of the altar. He then lays down a firebrand at the further (south) end of the line, For, were he to present that food to the Fathers without having laid down a firebrand, the Asuras and RÄkÅŸasas would certainly tamper with it. … He lays it down with the text ‘whatsoever Asuras roam about being attracted by the svadhÄ (offering to Fathers), assuming various shapes, be they large-bodied or small-bodied, may Agni expel them from this world (VÄj. S. II, 30)… He then takes the water jar and makes the Fathers wash (their hands) merely saying ‘N. N., wash thyself’ (naming) the sacrificer’s father; ‘N. N. wash thy self (naming the sacrificer’s) grandfather; ‘N. N., wash thyself

(naming) the sacrificer’s great-grandfather. As one would pour out water (for a guest) when he is about to take food, so in this case. Now those (stalks of sacrificial grass) are severed with one stroke and cut off near the root; the top belongs to the gods, the middle part to men and the root part (of darbhas) to the fathers. Therefore they are cut off near the roots …… He spreads them along with the line with their tops towards the south. Thereon he presents (to the fathers the three round cakes of rice). He presents thus :- for the gods they offer thus: for men they ladle out; and in the case of the fathers they do in this very way; therefore he presents (the cakes to the fathers) thus. With ‘N. N., this for thee!’ he presents one cake to the sacrificer’s father. Some add ‘for those that come after thee’, but let him not say this, since he himself is one of those to whom it would be offered in common. Let him therefore merely say ‘N, N., this for thee!’ as to the sacrificer’s father; ‘N. N., this for thee’ as to his grandfather; ‘N. N., this for thee’ as to his great grandfather…He then mutters ‘Here, O fathers, regale your selves; like bulls come hither each to his own share!’ (VÄj. S. II, 31). Whereby he says ‘Eat each his own share’. He then turns round to the left so as to face the opposite (north) side ; for the fathers are far away from men; and thereby he also is far away (from the fathers). ‘Let him remain (standing with bated breath) until his breath fail’ say some; ‘for thus far extends the vital energy’. However, having remained so far a moment-he again turns round (to the right) and mutters ’the Fathers have regaled themselves; like bulls they have come each to his own share’ (VÄj. S. II, 31); whereby what he says is ’they have eaten each his own share’. Thereupon he takes the water jar and makes them wash themselves (by pouring water on the piá¹‡á¸as), merely saying ‘N. N., wash thyself’ (naming) the sacrificer’s father; ‘N. N. wash thy self’ (naming) his grandfather; ‘N. N., wash thyself’ (naming) his paternal great-grandfather. Even as one would pour out (water for a guest). When he has taken his meal, so it is here. He then pulls down the tuck (of the sacrificer’s garment) and performs obeisance. The tuck is sacred to the Fathers; therefore he performs obeisance to them after pulling down the tuck, … Six times he performs obeisance, for there are six seasons and the fathers are the seasons. He mutters ‘Give us houses, O fathers’ for the fathers are the rulers of houses; and this is the prayer for blessing at this sacrificial performance. After the

Number of Ähutis

463

six with clarified butter. Two Ähutis only are mentioned by AÅ›v. Srauta II. 6. 12 (quoted in note 974), Asv. gx. It is here that various combinations of the order of the deities and of the words ‘svÄhÄ’ and ‘svadhÄ’ occur. The texts of a few works are cited below for illustration. Some give the order as ‘Soma accom panied by the pitá¹›s, Agni the carrier of kavya, Yama Angiras;’ while others give it as ‘Agni the carrier of kavya, Soma accompanied by the pitá¹›s, Yama Vaivasvata &c.’ It is further provided that the Ähuti to Agni is made to the southern side of the fire, to Soma on the northern part of it and in the middle of the two sides to Vaivasvata (Yama ).1033

IV]

Rules about serving food

465

side dishes viz. broths, vegetables, milk, curds, gheo and honey on the ground and not on a wooden stool or the like). The vessels placed on the ground should contain various kinds of food (such as sweetmeats, pÄyasa ), fruits and roots, delicious flesh, spiced or perfumed drinks. Having brought near the brÄhmaá¹‡as all the vessels containing food, he should serve it in the plates declaring the characteristic of each kind of food (saying this is sweet, this is sour’ &c.). The performer while serving should not shed tears (remembering his ancestors ), he should not become angry, should not utter falsehood, he should not touch the vessel containing ) food with his foot and should not serve food with a violent jerk. Whatever is liked by the brahmanas should be served to them without any grumbling and he should talk about Brahma (or about Vedic riddles ), because that is agreeable to the pitá¹›s. The performer himself, being pleased, should ( by his sweet speech ) gladden the brÄhmaá¹‡as, should make them partake of food without hurry, and should urge them on frequently (to take more food) by declaring the good points of the various items of food. All food should be steaming hot and the brÄhmaá¹‡as should partake of it silently and should not themselves speak of the good points of the food even when questioned by the performer. When the food is hot, when the brÄhmaá¹‡as eat in silence and when the good qualities of the food are not declared by the brÄhmaá¹‡as, the Fathers partake of it. When a brÄhmaá¹‡a (at a Å›rÄddha dinner) partakes of food, having his head covered with a garment (a turban &c )or facing the south or having his sandals on, evil spirits partake of the food (and not the Fathers).’ As early as Gaut., it is provided that the food for the brÄhmaá¹‡as should be of the highest sort and should be enriched by condiments and flavoured.

Some of the other rules are : The PrajÄpati-smá¹›ti (verses 57-62 ) states who should cook the food for the Å›rÄddha dinner. The wife, any fortunate (or handsome) woman of the performer’s gotra whose husband is living and who has a son or brother and is devoted to waiting upon her elders, the performer’s teacher’s wife, his maternal uncle’s wife, paternal or maternal aunt, his sister, his daughter or daughter-in-law, all of them having their husbands alive, should engage in the cooking, of the food for Å›rÄddha. It is now necessary to illustrate how pÄrvaá¹‡a Å›rÄddha is performed in modern times. Here the greatest variety prevails. The details of the rite vary in the different parts of India. They vary also according as the performer belongs to a particular Veda or a particular recension of the Veda, they vary according to the particular eminent writer that is followed and further additions are made according as the performer is a Vaispava or a Saiva. Such differences have to be passed over here, particularly because they are not material. We saw above that the PÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha was very simple in the days of the BrÄhmaá¹‡as and most of the sÅ«tras. There was then no express mention of the worship of ViÅ›vedevÄá¸¥ in PÄrvaá¹‡a Å›rÄddha, or of the maternal ancestors or of the wives of any ancestors. Gradually these came to be added and by the time of the YÄjÃ±avalkyasmá¹›ti there was special invocation of the ViÅ›vedevÄá¸¥. But these latter were probably not expressly divided into groups at the time of that work. The two meanings of sapiá¹‡á¸a given by the Mit, and the DÄyabhÄga have already been explained at great length in H. of Dh. vol. II, pp. 452-458 and pp. 472-477. The DÄyabhÄga pro pounded the theory that whoever conferred greater spiritual benefit on the deceased by the performance of Å›rÄddhas and the offering of piá¹‡á¸as was entitled to be preferred as an heir to the deceased’s wealth. The Mit. said that heirship depended on blood relationship and the nearest in blood to the deceased was the preferential heir. But even under the Mit. whoever took the wealth of the deceased was bound to pay his debts (Yaj. II, 51 ) and to offer Å›rÄddha and piá¹‡á¸a to him. This subject and the several propositions deduced from the texts have been dealt with in H. of Dh. vol. III, pp. 734-745.

One thing to be remembered is that in determining the preferential right regard is to be had to the capacity of a per son and the efficacy of the piá¹‡á¸as when offered. After a person takes the wealth of the deceased, there is no legal machinery to enforce his duty to offer piá¹‡á¸as 1138 It was argued in Nalinaksha v. Rajani Kanto 35 C. W. N. 726 that, if an heir does not offer piá¹‡á¸as or refuses to offer them to his ancestor. he was not entitled under Hindu Law to succeed to him. This argument was repelled by the judges with the remark that there was no authority for such a proposition and that the right to inheritance is based under the DÄyabhÄga not on offering piá¹‡á¸a but on the capacity to offer it (p. 729).   11. This idea is at least Indo-Iranian, if not Indo-European, The ancient Parsi scriptures speak of Fravashis that were originally the same as pitá¹›s of ancient Hindu works or the Manes among the ancient Romans. They were the everlasting and deified souls of the dead. Gradually the meaning of Fravashi was extended and even Gods and objects like the Earth and the sky were supposed to have each a Fravashi. Vide S. B. E. vol. 4 p. 262 for the Fravashis of the holy Yama and for the different classes of Fravashis; also S. B. E. vol. 23 pp. 180, 184, 230. ↩︎

  56. The Full Moon night of MÄgha is said to be the month of the year i. e. the year began on that day in ancient times.
